<th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background:#ddd"><a href="/wiki/Pharmacokinetics" title="Pharmacokinetics">Pharmacokinetic</a> data</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row" style="padding:0.2em 0; line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/Bioavailability" title="Bioavailability">Bioavailability</a></th>
<td>80–100%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSR_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-MSR-1">[1]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row" style="padding:0.2em 0; line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/Plasma_protein_binding" title="Plasma protein binding">Protein binding</a></th>
<td>80–90%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MD_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-MD-2">[2]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row" style="padding:0.2em 0; line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/Drug_metabolism" title="Drug metabolism">Metabolism</a></th>
<td><a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">Liver</a>, (<a href="/wiki/CYP2C19" title="CYP2C19">CYP2C19</a> and possibly <a href="/wiki/CYP3A" title="CYP3A">CYP3A</a>), some is also hydrolysed to salicylate in the gut wall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MD_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-MD-2">[2]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row" style="padding:0.2em 0; line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/Biological_half-life" title="Biological half-life">Biological half-life</a></th>
<td>Dose-dependent; 2 h to 3 h for low doses, 15 h to 30 h for large doses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MD_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-MD-2">[2]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row" style="padding:0.2em 0; line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/Excretion" title="Excretion">Excretion</a></th>
<td>Urine (80–100%), sweat, saliva, feces<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSR_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-MSR-1">[1]</a></sup></td>
</tr>

<tr>
<th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background:#ddd"><span style="font-weight:normal;">  <span class="reflink plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Special:ComparePages&amp;rev1=464364671&amp;page2=Aspirin">(verify)</a></span></span></th>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Aspirin</b>, also known as <b>acetylsalicylic acid</b> (<b>ASA</b>), is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medication" title="Medication">medication</a> used to treat <a href="/wiki/Pain" title="Pain">pain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fever" title="Fever">fever</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHSF2016_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-AHSF2016-3">[3]</a></sup> Specific inflammatory conditions in which it is used include <a href="/wiki/Kawasaki_disease" title="Kawasaki disease">Kawasaki disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pericarditis" title="Pericarditis">pericarditis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rheumatic_fever" title="Rheumatic fever">rheumatic fever</a>. Aspirin given shortly after a <a href="/wiki/Myocardial_infarction" title="Myocardial infarction">heart attack</a> decreases the risk of death. Aspirin is also used long-term to help prevent heart attacks, <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">strokes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thrombus" title="Thrombus">blood clots</a>, in people at high risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHSF2016_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-AHSF2016-3">[3]</a></sup> Aspirin may also decrease the risk of certain types of <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a>, particularly <a href="/wiki/Colorectal_cancer" title="Colorectal cancer">colorectal cancer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> For pain or fever, effects typically begin within 30 minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHSF2016_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-AHSF2016-3">[3]</a></sup> Aspirin is a <a href="/wiki/Nonsteroidal_anti-inflammatory_drug" title="Nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory drug">nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory drug</a> (NSAID) and works similar to other NSAIDs but it is also an <a href="/wiki/Antiplatelet_drug" title="Antiplatelet drug">antiplatelet</a> and suppresses the normal functioning of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Platelets" title="Platelets">platelets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHSF2016_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-AHSF2016-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Common side effects include an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upset_stomach" title="Upset stomach">upset stomach</a>. More significant side effects include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stomach_ulcer" title="Stomach ulcer">stomach ulcers</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stomach_bleeding" title="Stomach bleeding">stomach bleeding</a>, and worsening <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a>. Bleeding risk is greater among those who are older, drink <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohol</a>, take other NSAIDs, or are on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anticoagulants" title="Anticoagulants">blood thinners</a>. Aspirin is not recommended in the last part of <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">pregnancy</a>. It is not generally recommended in children with <a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">infections</a> because of the risk of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reye%27s_syndrome" title="Reye's syndrome">Reye's syndrome</a>. High doses may result in <a href="/wiki/Tinnitus" title="Tinnitus">ringing in the ears</a>.</p>
<p>Aspirin, in the form of leaves from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Willow_tree" title="Willow tree">willow tree</a>, has been used for its health effects for at least 2,400 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jon2015_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jon2015-5">[5]</a></sup> In 1853, chemist <a href="/wiki/Charles_Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Gerhardt" title="Charles Frédéric Gerhardt">Charles Frédéric Gerhardt</a> treated <a href="/wiki/Sodium_salicylate" title="Sodium salicylate">sodium salicylate</a> with <a href="/wiki/Acetyl_chloride" title="Acetyl chloride">acetyl chloride</a> to produce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acetylsalicylic_acid" title="Acetylsalicylic acid">acetylsalicylic acid</a> for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeffreys2008_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffreys2008-6">[6]</a></sup> In the second half of the nineteenth century, other chemists established the chemical structure and came up with more efficient methods to make it. In 1897, scientists at <a href="/wiki/Bayer" title="Bayer">Bayer</a> began studying acetylsalicylic acid as a less-irritating replacement for common salicylate medicines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeffreys2008_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffreys2008-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:69–75</sup> By 1899, Bayer had named the drug Aspirin and was selling it around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MannPlummer1991_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-MannPlummer1991-7">[7]</a></sup> The word <i>Aspirin</i> was Bayer's brand name; however, Bayer's rights to the trademark were lost or sold in many countries. Aspirin's popularity grew over the first half of the twentieth century leading to fierce competition with many aspirin brands and products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACS_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-ACS-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Aspirin is one of the most widely used medications globally with an estimated 40,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tonnes" title="Tonnes">tonnes</a> (50 to 120 billion pills) being consumed each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jon2015_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jon2015-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-COX2002_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-COX2002-9">[9]</a></sup> It is on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization%27s_List_of_Essential_Medicines" title="World Health Organization's List of Essential Medicines">World Health Organization's List of Essential Medicines</a>, the most effective and safe medicines needed in a <a href="/wiki/Health_system" title="Health system">health system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO19th_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO19th-10">[10]</a></sup> Aspirin is available as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Generic_medication" title="Generic medication">generic medication</a>. The wholesale cost in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_world" title="Developing world">developing world</a> as of 2014 is 0.002 to 0.025 USD per dose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> As of 2015 the cost for a typical month of medication in the United States is less than <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US$</a>25.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ric2015_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ric2015-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
