<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical_use">Medical use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Medical use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Aspirin is used in the treatment of a number of conditions, including fever, pain, <a href="/wiki/Rheumatic_fever" title="Rheumatic fever">rheumatic fever</a>, and inflammatory diseases, such as <a href="/wiki/Rheumatoid_arthritis" title="Rheumatoid arthritis">rheumatoid arthritis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pericarditis" title="Pericarditis">pericarditis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kawasaki_disease" title="Kawasaki disease">Kawasaki disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHFS_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-AHFS-13">[13]</a></sup> Lower doses of aspirin have also shown to reduce the risk of death from a <a href="/wiki/Myocardial_infarction" title="Myocardial infarction">heart attack</a>, or the risk of <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">stroke</a> in some circumstances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USFDA-patient-guideline_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-USFDA-patient-guideline-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USPSTF-CV_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-USPSTF-CV-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> There is some evidence that aspirin is effective at preventing <a href="/wiki/Colorectal_cancer" title="Colorectal cancer">colorectal cancer</a>, though the mechanisms of this effect are unclear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Algra_518.E2.80.9327_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Algra_518.E2.80.9327-17">[17]</a></sup> In the United States low dose aspirin is deemed reasonable in those between 50 and 70 years old who have a more than 10% risk of cardiovascular disease and are not at an increased risk of bleeding who are otherwise healthy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pain">Pain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Pain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aspirin1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1365" data-file-width="2037" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Aspirin1.jpg/220px-Aspirin1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Aspirin1.jpg/330px-Aspirin1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Aspirin1.jpg/440px-Aspirin1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aspirin1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Aspirin 325 mg / 5 grains for pain</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aspirine_macro_shot.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="1500" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Aspirine_macro_shot.jpg/220px-Aspirine_macro_shot.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Aspirine_macro_shot.jpg/330px-Aspirine_macro_shot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Aspirine_macro_shot.jpg/440px-Aspirine_macro_shot.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aspirine_macro_shot.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Uncoated aspirin <a href="/wiki/Tablet_(pharmacy)" title="Tablet (pharmacy)">tablets</a>, consisting of about 90% acetylsalicylic acid, along with a minor amount of inert fillers and binders</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Aspirin is an effective analgesic for acute pain, but is generally considered inferior to <a href="/wiki/Ibuprofen" title="Ibuprofen">ibuprofen</a> for the alleviation of pain because aspirin is more likely to cause <a href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_bleeding" title="Gastrointestinal bleeding">gastrointestinal bleeding</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15768621_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15768621-19">[19]</a></sup> Aspirin is generally ineffective for those pains caused by muscle <a href="/wiki/Cramp" title="Cramp">cramps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bloating" title="Bloating">bloating</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gastric_distension" title="Gastric distension">gastric distension</a>, or acute skin irritation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid14592563_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid14592563-20">[20]</a></sup> As with other NSAIDs, <a href="/wiki/Compound_analgesic" title="Compound analgesic">combinations</a> of aspirin and <a href="/wiki/Caffeine" title="Caffeine">caffeine</a> provide slightly greater pain relief than aspirin alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22419343_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22419343-21">[21]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Effervescent" title="Effervescent">Effervescent</a> formulations of aspirin, such as <a href="/wiki/Alka-Seltzer" title="Alka-Seltzer">Alka-Seltzer</a> or Blowfish,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USFDA_Blowfish_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-USFDA_Blowfish-22">[22]</a></sup> relieve pain faster than aspirin in tablets,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid10868553_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid10868553-23">[23]</a></sup> which makes them useful for the treatment of <a href="/wiki/Migraine" title="Migraine">migraines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18451718_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18451718-24">[24]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Topical_medication" title="Topical medication">Topical</a> aspirin may be effective for treating some types of <a href="/wiki/Neuropathic_pain" title="Neuropathic pain">neuropathic pain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Headache">Headache</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Headache">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Aspirin, either by itself or in a combined formulation, effectively treats certain <a href="/wiki/Headache#Classification" title="Headache">types of a headache</a>, but its efficacy may be questionable for others. Secondary headaches, meaning those caused by another disorder or trauma, should be promptly treated by a medical provider.</p>
<p>Among primary headaches, the <a href="/wiki/International_Classification_of_Headache_Disorders" title="International Classification of Headache Disorders">International Classification of Headache Disorders</a> distinguishes between <a href="/wiki/Tension_headache" title="Tension headache">tension headache</a> (the most common), migraine, and <a href="/wiki/Cluster_headache" title="Cluster headache">cluster headache</a>. Aspirin or other over-the-counter analgesics are widely recognized as effective for the treatment of tension headache.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>Aspirin, especially as a component of an <a href="/wiki/Aspirin/paracetamol/caffeine" title="Aspirin/paracetamol/caffeine">aspirin/paracetamol/caffeine</a> <a href="/wiki/Combination_drug" title="Combination drug">combination</a>, is considered a first-line therapy in the treatment of migraine, and comparable to lower doses of <a href="/wiki/Sumatriptan" title="Sumatriptan">sumatriptan</a>. It is most effective at stopping migraines when they are first beginning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fever">Fever</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Fever">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Like its ability to control pain, aspirin's ability to control <a href="/wiki/Fever" title="Fever">fever</a> is due to its action on the <a href="/wiki/Prostaglandin" title="Prostaglandin">prostaglandin</a> system through its irreversible inhibition of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/COX" title="COX">COX</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Although aspirin's use as an <a href="/wiki/Antipyretic" title="Antipyretic">antipyretic</a> in adults is well-established, many medical societies and regulatory agencies (including the <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Family_Physicians" title="American Academy of Family Physicians">American Academy of Family Physicians</a>, the <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Pediatrics" title="American Academy of Pediatrics">American Academy of Pediatrics</a>, and the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a> (FDA)) strongly advise against using aspirin for treatment of fever in children because of the risk of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reye%27s_syndrome" title="Reye's syndrome">Reye's syndrome</a>, a rare but often fatal illness associated with the use of aspirin or other salicylates in children during episodes of viral or bacterial infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AAPweb_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-AAPweb-31">[31]</a></sup> Because of the risk of Reye's syndrome in children, in 1986, the FDA required labeling on all aspirin-containing medications advising against its use in children and teenagers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FDA_1986_FR_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FDA_1986_FR-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inflammation">Inflammation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Inflammation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Aspirin is used as an anti-inflammatory agent for both acute and long-term inflammation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> as well as for treatment of inflammatory diseases, such as rheumatoid arthritis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHFS_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-AHFS-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heart_attacks_and_strokes">Heart attacks and strokes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Heart attacks and strokes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Aspirin is an important part of the treatment of those who have had a <a href="/wiki/Myocardial_infarction" title="Myocardial infarction">myocardial infarction</a> (heart attack).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> One trial found that among those likely having an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ST-segment_elevation_MI" title="ST-segment elevation MI">ST-segment elevation MI</a>, aspirin saves the life of 1 in 42 by reducing the 30-day death rate from 11.8% to 9.4%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Qu2009_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Qu2009-35">[35]</a></sup> There was no difference in major bleeding, but there was a small increase in minor bleeding amounting to roughly 1 in every 167 people given aspirin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Qu2009_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Qu2009-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="High_risk">High risk</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: High risk">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>For people who have already had a heart attack or stroke, taking aspirin daily for two years prevented 1 in 50 from having a cardiovascular problem (heart attack, stroke, or death), but also caused non-fatal bleeding problems to occur in 1 of 400 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20112887_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20112887-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19482214_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19482214-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lower_risk">Lower risk</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Lower risk">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Studies have not found an overall benefit in the general population of healthy people, although it is possible that there are small benefits for those at especially high risk, despite never having had a heart attack or stroke in the past.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> One study found that among those who have never had a heart attack or stroke, taking aspirin daily for 1 year prevents 1 in 1,667 from having a non-fatal heart attack or stroke, but caused 1 in 3,333 to have a non-fatal bleeding event. However, the study population were at relatively higher risk than those who had never had a heart attack or stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>Aspirin appears to offer little benefit to those at lower risk of heart attack or stroke—for instance, those without a history of these events or with pre-existing disease. Some studies recommend aspirin on a case-by-case basis,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19293073_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19293073-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USPSTF_aspirin_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-USPSTF_aspirin-42">[42]</a></sup> while others have suggested the risks of other events, such as gastrointestinal bleeding, were enough to outweigh any potential benefit, and recommended against using aspirin for primary prevention entirely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Aspirin has also been suggested as a component of a <a href="/wiki/Polypill" title="Polypill">polypill</a> for prevention of cardiovascular disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16100022_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16100022-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16603580_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16603580-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>Complicating the use of aspirin for prevention is the phenomenon of aspirin resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16364973_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16364973-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20944898_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20944898-47">[47]</a></sup> For people who are resistant, aspirin's efficacy is reduced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21306212_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21306212-48">[48]</a></sup> Some authors have suggested testing regimens to identify people who are resistant to aspirin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19576352_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19576352-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="After_surgery">After surgery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: After surgery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>After <a href="/wiki/Percutaneous_coronary_intervention" title="Percutaneous coronary intervention">percutaneous coronary interventions</a> (PCIs), such as the placement of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coronary_artery" title="Coronary artery">coronary artery</a> <a href="/wiki/Stent" title="Stent">stent</a>, a U.S. <a href="/wiki/Agency_for_Healthcare_Research_and_Quality" title="Agency for Healthcare Research and Quality">Agency for Healthcare Research and Quality</a> guideline recommends that aspirin be taken indefinitely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Frequently, aspirin is combined with an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ADP_receptor_inhibitor" title="ADP receptor inhibitor">ADP receptor inhibitor</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Clopidogrel" title="Clopidogrel">clopidogrel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prasugrel" title="Prasugrel">prasugrel</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Ticagrelor" title="Ticagrelor">ticagrelor</a> to prevent <a href="/wiki/Thrombosis" title="Thrombosis">blood clots</a>. This is called dual <a href="/wiki/Antiplatelet_drug" title="Antiplatelet drug">antiplatelet therapy</a> (DAPT). United States and European Union guidelines disagree somewhat about how long, and for what indications this combined therapy should be continued after surgery. U.S. guidelines recommend DAPT for at least 12 months, while EU guidelines recommend DAPT for 6–12 months after a drug-eluting stent placement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> However, they agree that aspirin be continued indefinitely after DAPT is complete.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cancer_prevention">Cancer prevention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Cancer prevention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Aspirin is thought to reduce the overall risk of both getting cancer and dying from cancer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cuz2014_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cuz2014-52">[52]</a></sup> This effect is particularly beneficial for <a href="/wiki/Colorectal_cancer" title="Colorectal cancer">colorectal cancer</a> (CRC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Algra_518.E2.80.9327_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Algra_518.E2.80.9327-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> It may also slightly reduce the risk of <a href="/wiki/Endometrial_cancer" title="Endometrial cancer">endometrial cancer</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Breast_cancer" title="Breast cancer">breast cancer</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Prostate_cancer" title="Prostate cancer">prostate cancer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some conclude the benefits are greater than the risks due to bleeding in those at average risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cuz2014_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cuz2014-52">[52]</a></sup> Others are unclear if the benefits are greater than the risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Given this uncertainty, the 2007 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Preventive_Services_Task_Force" title="United States Preventive Services Task Force">United States Preventive Services Task Force</a> guidelines on this topic recommended against the use of aspirin for prevention of CRC in people with average risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_uses">Other uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Other uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Aspirin is a first-line treatment for the fever and joint-pain symptoms of <a href="/wiki/Rheumatic_fever" title="Rheumatic fever">acute rheumatic fever</a>. The therapy often lasts for one to two weeks, and is rarely indicated for longer periods. After fever and pain have subsided, the aspirin is no longer necessary, since it does not decrease the incidence of heart complications and residual rheumatic heart disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHFA_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-NHFA-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Naproxen" title="Naproxen">Naproxen</a> has been shown to be as effective as aspirin and less toxic, but due to the limited clinical experience, naproxen is recommended only as a second-line treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHFA_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-NHFA-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>Along with rheumatic fever, <a href="/wiki/Kawasaki_disease" title="Kawasaki disease">Kawasaki disease</a> remains one of the few indications for aspirin use in children<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> in spite of a lack of high quality evidence for its effectiveness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>Low-dose aspirin supplementation has moderate benefits when used for prevention of <a href="/wiki/Pre-eclampsia" title="Pre-eclampsia">pre-eclampsia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duley_2007_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Duley_2007-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roberge_2012_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roberge_2012-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Resistance">Resistance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Resistance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For some people, aspirin does not have as strong an effect on platelets as for others, an effect known as aspirin-resistance or insensitivity. One study has suggested women are more likely to be resistant than men,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> and a different, aggregate study of 2,930 people found 28% were resistant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18202034_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18202034-69">[69]</a></sup> A study in 100 Italian people, though, found, of the apparent 31% aspirin-resistant subjects, only 5% were truly resistant, and the others were <a href="/wiki/Compliance_(medicine)" title="Compliance (medicine)">noncompliant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18680540_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18680540-70">[70]</a></sup> Another study of 400 healthy volunteers found no subjects who were truly resistant, but some had "pseudoresistance, reflecting delayed and reduced drug absorption".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source(s). (August 2016)">verification needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dosage">Dosage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Dosage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Regular_strength_enteric_coated_aspirin_tablets.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1432" data-file-width="2148" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Regular_strength_enteric_coated_aspirin_tablets.jpg/220px-Regular_strength_enteric_coated_aspirin_tablets.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Regular_strength_enteric_coated_aspirin_tablets.jpg/330px-Regular_strength_enteric_coated_aspirin_tablets.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Regular_strength_enteric_coated_aspirin_tablets.jpg/440px-Regular_strength_enteric_coated_aspirin_tablets.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Regular_strength_enteric_coated_aspirin_tablets.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Coated 325 mg (5-grain) aspirin tablets</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:5_grain_aspirin.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="642" data-file-width="1473" height="109" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/5_grain_aspirin.jpg/250px-5_grain_aspirin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/5_grain_aspirin.jpg/375px-5_grain_aspirin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/5_grain_aspirin.jpg/500px-5_grain_aspirin.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:5_grain_aspirin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The 5-grain aspirin. The usage guidance label on a bottle of aspirin indicates that the dosage is "325 mg (5 gr)".</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Adult aspirin tablets are produced in standardised sizes, which vary slightly from country to country, for example 300 mg in Britain and 325 mg (or 5 <a href="/wiki/Grain_(unit)" title="Grain (unit)">grains</a>) in the United States. Smaller doses are based on these standards, <i>e.g.</i>, 75 mg and 81 mg tablets. The 81 mg (1<span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>4</sub></span>-grain) tablets are commonly called "baby aspirin" or "baby-strength", because they were originally—but no longer—intended to be administered to infants and children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> No medical significance occurs due to the slight difference in dosage between the 75 mg and the 81 mg tablets.</p>
<p>In general, for adults, doses are taken four times a day for fever or arthritis,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BNF_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-BNF-73">[73]</a></sup> with doses near the maximal daily dose used historically for the treatment of rheumatic fever.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> For the prevention of myocardial infarction (MI) in someone with documented or suspected coronary artery disease, much lower doses are taken once daily.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BNF_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-BNF-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>Recommendations from the USPSTF<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> on the use of aspirin for the primary prevention of coronary heart disease encourage men aged 45–79 and women aged 55–79 to use aspirin when the potential benefit of a reduction in MI for men or stroke for women outweighs the potential harm of an increase in gastrointestinal hemorrhage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-medscape_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-medscape-76">[76]</a></sup> The WHI study said regular low dose (75 or 81 mg) aspirin female users had a 25% lower risk of death from cardiovascular disease and a 14% lower risk of death from any cause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-medscape_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-medscape-76">[76]</a></sup> Low-dose aspirin use was also associated with a trend toward lower risk of cardiovascular events, and lower aspirin doses (75 or 81 mg/day) may optimize efficacy and safety for people requiring aspirin for long-term prevention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-medscape_76-2"><a href="#cite_note-medscape-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>In children with Kawasaki disease, aspirin is taken at dosages based on body weight, initially four times a day for up to two weeks and then at a lower dose once daily for a further six to eight weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Adverse_effects">Adverse effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Adverse effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contraindications">Contraindications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Contraindications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Aspirin should not be taken by people who are allergic to <a href="/wiki/Ibuprofen" title="Ibuprofen">ibuprofen</a> or <a href="/wiki/Naproxen" title="Naproxen">naproxen</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drugs.com_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-drugs.com-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-personalmd_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-personalmd-79">[79]</a></sup> or who have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salicylate_intolerance" title="Salicylate intolerance">salicylate intolerance</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16247191_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16247191-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid8566739_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid8566739-81">[81]</a></sup> or a more generalized <a href="/wiki/Drug_intolerance" title="Drug intolerance">drug intolerance</a> to NSAIDs, and caution should be exercised in those with <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a> or NSAID-precipitated <a href="/wiki/Bronchospasm" title="Bronchospasm">bronchospasm</a>. Owing to its effect on the stomach lining, manufacturers recommend people with <a href="/wiki/Peptic_ulcer" title="Peptic ulcer">peptic ulcers</a>, mild <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Gastritis" title="Gastritis">gastritis</a> seek medical advice before using aspirin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drugs.com_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-drugs.com-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mercksource_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-mercksource-82">[82]</a></sup> Even if none of these conditions is present, the risk of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_hemorrhage" title="Gastrointestinal hemorrhage">stomach bleeding</a> is still increased when aspirin is taken with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcoholic_beverage" title="Alcoholic beverage">alcohol</a> or <a href="/wiki/Warfarin" title="Warfarin">warfarin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drugs.com_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-drugs.com-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-personalmd_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-personalmd-79">[79]</a></sup> People with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemophilia" title="Hemophilia">hemophilia</a> or other bleeding tendencies should not take aspirin or other salicylates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drugs.com_78-3"><a href="#cite_note-drugs.com-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mercksource_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-mercksource-82">[82]</a></sup> Aspirin is known to cause <a href="/wiki/Hemolytic_anemia" title="Hemolytic anemia">hemolytic anemia</a> in people who have the genetic disease <a href="/wiki/Glucose-6-phosphate_dehydrogenase_deficiency" title="Glucose-6-phosphate dehydrogenase deficiency">glucose-6-phosphate dehydrogenase deficiency</a>, particularly in large doses and depending on the severity of the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Use of aspirin during <a href="/wiki/Dengue_fever" title="Dengue fever">dengue fever</a> is not recommended owing to increased bleeding tendency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> People with <a href="/wiki/Kidney_disease" title="Kidney disease">kidney disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hyperuricemia" title="Hyperuricemia">hyperuricemia</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Gout" title="Gout">gout</a> should not take aspirin because it inhibits the kidneys' ability to excrete <a href="/wiki/Uric_acid" title="Uric acid">uric acid</a>, thus may exacerbate these conditions. Aspirin should not be given to children or adolescents to control cold or influenza symptoms, as this has been linked with <a href="/wiki/Reye_syndrome" title="Reye syndrome">Reye's syndrome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BMJ2002-Macdonald_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-BMJ2002-Macdonald-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gastrointestinal">Gastrointestinal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Gastrointestinal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Aspirin use has been shown to increase the risk of gastrointestinal bleeding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H_Toft_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-H_Toft-86">[86]</a></sup> Although some <a href="/wiki/Enteric_coating" title="Enteric coating">enteric-coated</a> formulations of aspirin are advertised as being "gentle to the stomach", in one study, enteric coating did not seem to reduce this risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H_Toft_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-H_Toft-86">[86]</a></sup> Combining aspirin with other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NSAID" title="NSAID">NSAIDs</a> has also been shown to further increase this risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H_Toft_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-H_Toft-86">[86]</a></sup> Using aspirin in combination with clopidogrel or warfarin also increases the risk of upper gastrointestinal bleeding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>Blockade of COX-1 by aspirin apparently results in the upregulation of COX-2 as part of a gastric defense<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> and that taking COX-2 inhibitors concurrently with aspirin increases the gastric mucosal erosion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Therefore, caution should be exercised if combining aspirin with any "natural" supplements with COX-2-inhibiting properties, such as garlic extracts, curcumin, bilberry, pine bark, ginkgo, fish oil, resveratrol, genistein, quercetin, resorcinol, and others.</p>
<p>In addition to enteric coating, "buffering" is the other main method companies have used to try to mitigate the problem of gastrointestinal bleeding. Buffering agents are intended to work by preventing the aspirin from concentrating in the walls of the stomach, although the benefits of buffered aspirin are disputed. Almost any buffering agent used in antacids can be used; Bufferin, for example, uses <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_oxide" title="Magnesium oxide">magnesium oxide</a>. Other preparations use <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcium carbonate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>Taking it with vitamin C is a more recently investigated method of protecting the stomach lining. Taking equal doses of vitamin C and aspirin may decrease the amount of stomach damage that occurs compared to taking aspirin alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dammann_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dammann-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Konturek_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Konturek-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_effects">Central effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Central effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Large doses of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salicylate" title="Salicylate">salicylate</a>, a metabolite of aspirin, cause temporary <a href="/wiki/Tinnitus" title="Tinnitus">tinnitus</a> (ringing in the ears) based on experiments in rats, via the action on <a href="/wiki/Arachidonic_acid" title="Arachidonic acid">arachidonic acid</a> and <a href="/wiki/NMDA_receptor" title="NMDA receptor">NMDA receptors</a> cascade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gutton_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gutton-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reye.27s_syndrome">Reye's syndrome</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Reye's syndrome">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reye%27s_syndrome" title="Reye's syndrome">Reye's syndrome</a></div>
<p>Reye's syndrome, a rare but severe illness characterized by acute <a href="/wiki/Encephalopathy" title="Encephalopathy">encephalopathy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fatty_liver" title="Fatty liver">fatty liver</a>, can occur when children or adolescents are given aspirin for a fever or other illness or infection. From 1981 through 1997, 1207 cases of Reye's syndrome in people younger than 18 were reported to the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">Centers for Disease Control and Prevention</a>. Of these, 93% reported being ill in the three weeks preceding the onset of Reye's syndrome, most commonly with a <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_tract_infection" title="Respiratory tract infection">respiratory infection</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chickenpox" title="Chickenpox">chickenpox</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">diarrhea</a>. Salicylates were detectable in 81.9% of children for whom test results were reported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belay_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Belay-94">[94]</a></sup> After the association between Reye's syndrome and aspirin was reported, and safety measures to prevent it (including a <a href="/wiki/Surgeon_General_of_the_United_States" title="Surgeon General of the United States">Surgeon General</a>'s warning, and changes to the labeling of aspirin-containing drugs) were implemented, aspirin taken by children declined considerably in the United States, as did the number of reported cases of Reye's syndrome; a similar decline was found in the United Kingdom after warnings against pediatric aspirin use were issued.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belay_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Belay-94">[94]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">U.S. Food and Drug Administration</a> now recommends aspirin (or aspirin-containing products) should not be given to anyone under the age of 12 who has a fever,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BMJ2002-Macdonald_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-BMJ2002-Macdonald-85">[85]</a></sup> and the British <a href="/wiki/Medicines_and_Healthcare_products_Regulatory_Agency" title="Medicines and Healthcare products Regulatory Agency">Medicines and Healthcare products Regulatory Agency</a> recommends children who are under 16 years of age should not take aspirin, unless it is on the advice of a doctor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Skin">Skin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Skin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For a small number of people, taking aspirin can result in symptoms resembling an allergic reaction, including <a href="/wiki/Hives" title="Hives">hives</a>, swelling, and headache. The reaction is caused by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salicylate_intolerance" title="Salicylate intolerance">salicylate intolerance</a> and is not a true <a href="/wiki/Allergy" title="Allergy">allergy</a>, but rather an inability to metabolize even small amounts of aspirin, resulting in an <a href="#Overdose">overdose</a>.</p>
<p>Aspirin and other NSAIDs, such as ibuprofen, may delay the healing of skin wounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Aspirin may however help heal venous leg ulcers that have not healed following usual treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_adverse_effects">Other adverse effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Other adverse effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Aspirin can induce <a href="/wiki/Angioedema" title="Angioedema">swelling of skin tissues</a> in some people. In one study, angioedema appeared one to six hours after ingesting aspirin in some of the people. However, when the aspirin was taken alone, it did not cause angioedema in these people; the aspirin had been taken in combination with another NSAID-induced drug when angioedema appeared.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>Aspirin causes an increased risk of cerebral microbleeds having the appearance on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MRI" title="MRI">MRI</a> scans of 5 to 10 mm or smaller, hypointense (dark holes) patches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Such cerebral microbleeds are important, since they often occur prior to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ischemic_stroke" title="Ischemic stroke">ischemic stroke</a> or <a href="/wiki/Intracerebral_hemorrhage" title="Intracerebral hemorrhage">intracerebral hemorrhage</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Binswanger_disease" title="Binswanger disease">Binswanger disease</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_disease" title="Alzheimer's disease">Alzheimer's disease</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (March 2011)">original research?</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>A study of a group with a mean dosage of aspirin of 270 mg per day estimated an average absolute risk increase in <a href="/wiki/Intracerebral_hemorrhage" title="Intracerebral hemorrhage">intracerebral hemorrhage</a> (ICH) of 12 events per 10,000 persons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-He1998_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-He1998-101">[101]</a></sup> In comparison, the estimated absolute risk reduction in myocardial infarction was 137 events per 10,000 persons, and a reduction of 39 events per 10,000 persons in ischemic stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-He1998_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-He1998-101">[101]</a></sup> In cases where ICH already has occurred, aspirin use results in higher mortality, with a dose of about 250 mg per day resulting in a <a href="/wiki/Relative_risk" title="Relative risk">relative risk</a> of death within three months after the ICH around 2.5 (95% <a href="/wiki/Confidence_interval" title="Confidence interval">confidence interval</a> 1.3 to 4.6).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saloheimo2006_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saloheimo2006-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>Aspirin and other NSAIDs can cause <a href="/wiki/Hyperkalemia" title="Hyperkalemia">abnormally high blood levels of potassium</a> by inducing a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyporeninemic_hypoaldosteronism" title="Hyporeninemic hypoaldosteronism">hyporeninemic hypoaldosteronic state</a> via inhibition of prostaglandin synthesis; however, these agents do not typically cause hyperkalemia by themselves in the setting of normal renal function and euvolemic state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>Aspirin can cause prolonged bleeding after operations for up to 10 days. In one study, 30 of 6499 people having elective surgery required reoperations to control bleeding. Twenty had diffuse bleeding and 10 had bleeding from a site. Diffuse, but not discrete, bleeding was associated with the preoperative use of aspirin alone or in combination with other NSAIDS in 19 of the 20 diffuse bleeding people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 9 July 2015, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FDA" title="FDA">FDA</a> toughened warnings of increased <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_attack" title="Heart attack">heart attack</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">stroke</a> risk associated with <a href="/wiki/Nonsteroidal_anti-inflammatory_drug" title="Nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory drug">nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory drugs</a> (NSAID). Aspirin is an NSAID but is not affected by the new warnings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FDA-20150709_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FDA-20150709-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Overdose">Overdose</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Overdose">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Aspirin_poisoning" title="Aspirin poisoning">Aspirin poisoning</a></div>
<p>Aspirin overdose can be acute or chronic. In acute poisoning, a single large dose is taken; in chronic poisoning, higher than normal doses are taken over a period of time. Acute overdose has a <a href="/wiki/Mortality_rate" title="Mortality rate">mortality rate</a> of 2%. Chronic overdose is more commonly lethal, with a mortality rate of 25%;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> chronic overdose may be especially severe in children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pediatrics1982-gaudreault_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pediatrics1982-gaudreault-107">[107]</a></sup> Toxicity is managed with a number of potential treatments, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Activated_charcoal" title="Activated charcoal">activated charcoal</a>, intravenous dextrose and normal saline, <a href="/wiki/Sodium_bicarbonate" title="Sodium bicarbonate">sodium bicarbonate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dialysis" title="Dialysis">dialysis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> The diagnosis of poisoning usually involves measurement of plasma salicylate, the active metabolite of aspirin, by automated spectrophotometric methods. Plasma salicylate levels in general range from 30–100 mg/l after usual therapeutic doses, 50–300 mg/l in people taking high doses and 700–1400 mg/l following acute overdose. Salicylate is also produced as a result of exposure to <a href="/wiki/Bismuth_subsalicylate" title="Bismuth subsalicylate">bismuth subsalicylate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methyl_salicylate" title="Methyl salicylate">methyl salicylate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sodium_salicylate" title="Sodium salicylate">sodium salicylate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interactions">Interactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Interactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Aspirin is known to <a href="/wiki/Drug_interaction" title="Drug interaction">interact</a> with other drugs. For example, <a href="/wiki/Acetazolamide" title="Acetazolamide">acetazolamide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_chloride" title="Ammonium chloride">ammonium chloride</a> are known to enhance the intoxicating effect of salicylates, and alcohol also increases the gastrointestinal bleeding associated with these types of drugs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drugs.com_78-4"><a href="#cite_note-drugs.com-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-personalmd_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-personalmd-79">[79]</a></sup> Aspirin is known to displace a number of drugs from protein-binding sites in the blood, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antidiabetic_drug" title="Antidiabetic drug">antidiabetic drugs</a> <a href="/wiki/Tolbutamide" title="Tolbutamide">tolbutamide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chlorpropamide" title="Chlorpropamide">chlorpropamide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Warfarin" title="Warfarin">warfarin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methotrexate" title="Methotrexate">methotrexate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phenytoin" title="Phenytoin">phenytoin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Probenecid" title="Probenecid">probenecid</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valproic_acid" title="Valproic acid">valproic acid</a> (as well as interfering with <a href="/wiki/Beta_oxidation" title="Beta oxidation">beta oxidation</a>, an important part of valproate metabolism), and other NSAIDs. Corticosteroids may also reduce the concentration of aspirin. Ibuprofen can negate the antiplatelet effect of aspirin used for cardioprotection and stroke prevention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FDA2006_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FDA2006-111">[111]</a></sup> The pharmacological activity of <a href="/wiki/Spironolactone" title="Spironolactone">spironolactone</a> may be reduced by taking aspirin, and it is known to compete with <a href="/wiki/Penicillin" title="Penicillin">penicillin G</a> for renal tubular secretion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-interactions_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-interactions-112">[112]</a></sup> Aspirin may also inhibit the absorption of vitamin C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources_(medicine)" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources (medicine)"><span title="Material near this tag may rely on an unreliable or less reliable medical source. (August 2016)">unreliable medical source?</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_properties">Chemical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Chemical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Aspirin decomposes rapidly in solutions of <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_acetate" title="Ammonium acetate">ammonium acetate</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Acetate" title="Acetate">acetates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonates</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Citrate" title="Citrate">citrates</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Hydroxide" title="Hydroxide">hydroxides</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkali_metals" title="Alkali metals">alkali metals</a>. It is stable in dry air, but gradually <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrolyses" title="Hydrolyses">hydrolyses</a> in contact with moisture to <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Salicylic_acid" title="Salicylic acid">salicylic acids</a>. In solution with alkalis, the hydrolysis proceeds rapidly and the clear solutions formed may consist entirely of acetate and salicylate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>Like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flour_mill" title="Flour mill">flour mills</a>, factories that make aspirin tablets must pay attention to how much of the powder gets into the air inside the building, because <a href="/wiki/Dust_explosion" title="Dust explosion">the powder-air mixture can be explosive</a>. The <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_for_Occupational_Safety_and_Health" title="National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health">National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health</a> (NIOSH) has set a <a href="/wiki/Recommended_exposure_limit" title="Recommended exposure limit">recommended exposure limit</a> in the United States of 5 mg/m<sup>3</sup> (time-weighted average).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> In 1989, the <a href="/wiki/Occupational_Safety_and_Health_Administration" title="Occupational Safety and Health Administration">Occupational Safety and Health Administration</a> set a legal <a href="/wiki/Permissible_exposure_limit" title="Permissible exposure limit">permissible exposure limit</a> for aspirin of 5 mg/m<sup>3</sup>, but this was vacated by the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=AFL-CIO_v._OSHA&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="AFL-CIO v. OSHA (page does not exist)">AFL-CIO v. OSHA</a> decision in 1993.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Synthesis">Synthesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Synthesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The synthesis of aspirin is classified as an <a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">esterification</a> reaction. <a href="/wiki/Salicylic_acid" title="Salicylic acid">Salicylic acid</a> is treated with <a href="/wiki/Acetic_anhydride" title="Acetic anhydride">acetic anhydride</a>, an acid derivative, causing a chemical reaction that turns salicylic acid's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroxyl" title="Hydroxyl">hydroxyl</a> group into an <a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">ester</a> group (R-OH → R-OCOCH<sub>3</sub>). This process yields aspirin and <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a>, which is considered a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byproduct" title="Byproduct">byproduct</a> of this reaction. Small amounts of <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> (and occasionally <a href="/wiki/Phosphoric_acid" title="Phosphoric acid">phosphoric acid</a>) are almost always used as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a>. This method is commonly employed in undergraduate teaching labs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aspirin_synthesis.png"><img alt="Aspirin synthesis.png" data-file-height="278" data-file-width="1668" height="82" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Aspirin_synthesis.png/490px-Aspirin_synthesis.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Aspirin_synthesis.png/735px-Aspirin_synthesis.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Aspirin_synthesis.png/980px-Aspirin_synthesis.png 2x" width="490"/></a></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>Reaction mechanism</dt>
<dd><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Acetylation_of_salicylic_acid,_mechanism.png" title="Acetylation of salicylic acid, mechanism"><img alt="Acetylation of salicylic acid, mechanism" data-file-height="832" data-file-width="3056" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Acetylation_of_salicylic_acid%2C_mechanism.png/800px-Acetylation_of_salicylic_acid%2C_mechanism.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Acetylation_of_salicylic_acid%2C_mechanism.png/1200px-Acetylation_of_salicylic_acid%2C_mechanism.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Acetylation_of_salicylic_acid%2C_mechanism.png/1600px-Acetylation_of_salicylic_acid%2C_mechanism.png 2x" width="800"/></a></dd>
</dl>
<p>Formulations containing high concentrations of aspirin often smell like <a href="/wiki/Vinegar" title="Vinegar">vinegar</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> because aspirin can decompose through hydrolysis in moist conditions, yielding salicylic and acetic acids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Physical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Aspirin, an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acetyl" title="Acetyl">acetyl</a> derivative of salicylic acid, is a white, crystalline, weakly acidic substance, with a <a href="/wiki/Melting_point" title="Melting point">melting point</a> of 136 °C (277 °F), and a boiling point of 140 °C (284 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Myers2007_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Myers2007-122">[122]</a></sup> Its acid dissociation constant (<a href="/wiki/Acid_dissociation_constant" title="Acid dissociation constant">pK<sub>a</sub></a>) is 3.5 at 25 °C (77 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asaaciddissconst_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-asaaciddissconst-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polymorphism">Polymorphism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Polymorphism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Polymorphism_(materials_science)" title="Polymorphism (materials science)">Polymorphism</a>, or the ability of a substance to form more than one <a href="/wiki/Crystal_structure" title="Crystal structure">crystal structure</a>, is important in the development of pharmaceutical ingredients. Many drugs are receiving regulatory approval for only a single crystal form or polymorph. For a long time, only one crystal structure for aspirin was known. That aspirin might have a second crystalline form was suspected since the 1960s. The elusive second polymorph was first discovered by Vishweshwar and coworkers in 2005,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> and fine structural details were given by Bond <i>et al.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> A new crystal type was found after attempted cocrystallization of aspirin and <a href="/wiki/Levetiracetam" title="Levetiracetam">levetiracetam</a> from hot <a href="/wiki/Acetonitrile" title="Acetonitrile">acetonitrile</a>. The form II is only stable at 100<span class="nowrap"> </span><a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a> and reverts to form I at ambient temperature. In the (unambiguous) form I, two salicylic molecules form centrosymmetric <a href="/wiki/Dimer_(chemistry)" title="Dimer (chemistry)">dimers</a> through the acetyl groups with the (acidic) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methyl" title="Methyl">methyl</a> proton to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbonyl" title="Carbonyl">carbonyl</a> <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bond" title="Hydrogen bond">hydrogen bonds</a>, and in the newly claimed form II, each salicylic molecule forms the same hydrogen bonds with two neighboring molecules instead of one. With respect to the hydrogen bonds formed by the <a href="/wiki/Carboxylic_acid" title="Carboxylic acid">carboxylic acid</a> groups, both polymorphs form identical dimer structures.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanism_of_action">Mechanism of action</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Mechanism of action">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mechanism_of_action_of_aspirin" title="Mechanism of action of aspirin">Mechanism of action of aspirin</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_of_the_mechanism">Discovery of the mechanism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Discovery of the mechanism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1971, British <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pharmacologist" title="Pharmacologist">pharmacologist</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Robert_Vane" title="John Robert Vane">John Robert Vane</a>, then employed by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_College_of_Surgeons_of_England" title="Royal College of Surgeons of England">Royal College of Surgeons</a> in London, showed aspirin suppressed the production of <a href="/wiki/Prostaglandin" title="Prostaglandin">prostaglandins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thromboxane" title="Thromboxane">thromboxanes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> For this discovery he was awarded the 1982 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine">Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine</a>, jointly with <a href="/wiki/Sune_Bergstr%C3%B6m" title="Sune Bergström">Sune Bergström</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bengt_Ingemar_Samuelsson" title="Bengt Ingemar Samuelsson">Bengt Ingemar Samuelsson</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> In 1984, he was made a <a href="/wiki/Knight_Bachelor" title="Knight Bachelor">Knight Bachelor</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prostaglandins_and_thromboxanes">Prostaglandins and thromboxanes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Prostaglandins and thromboxanes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Aspirin's ability to suppress the production of prostaglandins and thromboxanes is due to its irreversible inactivation of the <a href="/wiki/Cyclooxygenase" title="Cyclooxygenase">cyclooxygenase</a> (COX; officially known as prostaglandin-endoperoxide synthase, PTGS) enzyme required for prostaglandin and thromboxane synthesis. Aspirin acts as an acetylating agent where an acetyl group is covalently attached to a <a href="/wiki/Serine" title="Serine">serine</a> residue in the active site of the PTGS enzyme. This makes aspirin different from other NSAIDs (such as <a href="/wiki/Diclofenac" title="Diclofenac">diclofenac</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ibuprofen" title="Ibuprofen">ibuprofen</a>), which are reversible inhibitors (<a href="/wiki/Suicide_inhibition" title="Suicide inhibition">Suicide inhibition</a>).</p>
<p>Low-dose aspirin use irreversibly blocks the formation of <a href="/wiki/Thromboxane_A2" title="Thromboxane A2">thromboxane A<sub>2</sub></a> in platelets, producing an inhibitory effect on platelet aggregation during the lifetime of the affected platelet (8–9 days). This antithrombotic property makes aspirin useful for reducing the incidence of heart attacks in people who have had a heart attack, unstable angina, ischemic stroke or transient ischemic attack.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> 40 mg of aspirin a day is able to inhibit a large proportion of maximum thromboxane A<sub>2</sub> release provoked acutely, with the prostaglandin I2 synthesis being little affected; however, higher doses of aspirin are required to attain further inhibition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>Prostaglandins, local <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">hormones</a> produced in the body, have diverse effects, including the transmission of pain information to the brain, modulation of the <a href="/wiki/Hypothalamus" title="Hypothalamus">hypothalamic</a> thermostat, and inflammation. Thromboxanes are responsible for the aggregation of platelets that form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clot" title="Clot">blood clots</a>. Heart attacks are caused primarily by blood clots, and low doses of aspirin are seen as an effective medical intervention for acute myocardial infarction.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="COX-1_and_COX-2_inhibition">COX-1 and COX-2 inhibition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: COX-1 and COX-2 inhibition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At least two different types of cyclooxygenases, COX-1 and COX-2, are acted on by aspirin. Aspirin irreversibly inhibits COX-1 and modifies the enzymatic activity of COX-2. COX-2 normally produces prostanoids, most of which are proinflammatory. Aspirin-modified PTGS2 produces lipoxins, most of which are anti-inflammatory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source(s). (August 2016)">verification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Newer NSAID drugs, <a href="/wiki/COX-2_inhibitor" title="COX-2 inhibitor">COX-2 inhibitors</a> (coxibs), have been developed to inhibit only PTGS2, with the intent to reduce the incidence of gastrointestinal side effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-COX2002_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-COX2002-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, several of the new COX-2 inhibitors, such as <a href="/wiki/Rofecoxib" title="Rofecoxib">rofecoxib</a> (Vioxx), have been withdrawn in the last decade, after evidence emerged that PTGS2 inhibitors increase the risk of heart attack and stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> Endothelial cells lining the microvasculature in the body are proposed to express PTGS2, and, by selectively inhibiting PTGS2, prostaglandin production (specifically, PGI2; prostacyclin) is downregulated with respect to thromboxane levels, as PTGS1 in platelets is unaffected. Thus, the protective anticoagulative effect of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PGI2" title="PGI2">PGI2</a> is removed, increasing the risk of thrombus and associated heart attacks and other circulatory problems. Since platelets have no DNA, they are unable to synthesize new PTGS once aspirin has irreversibly inhibited the enzyme, an important difference with reversible inhibitors.</p>
<p>Furthermore, aspirin, while inhibiting the ability of COX-2 to form pro-inflammatory products such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prostaglandins" title="Prostaglandins">prostaglandins</a>, converts this enzyme's activity from a prostaglandin-forming cyclooxygenase to a <a href="/wiki/Lipoxygenase" title="Lipoxygenase">lipoxygenase</a>-like enzyme: aspirin-treated COX-2 metabolizes a variety of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyunsaturated_fatty_acids" title="Polyunsaturated fatty acids">polyunsaturated fatty acids</a> to hydroperoxy products which are then further metabolized to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specialized_proresolving_mediators" title="Specialized proresolving mediators">specialized proresolving mediators</a> such as the aspirin-triggered <a href="/wiki/Lipoxin" title="Lipoxin">lipoxins</a>, aspirin-triggered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Resolvins" title="Resolvins">resolvins</a>, and aspirin-triggered <a href="/wiki/Maresin" title="Maresin">maresins</a>. These mediators possess potent anti-inflammatory activity. It is proposed that this aspirin-triggered transition of COX-2 from cyclooxygenase to lipoxygenase activity and the consequential formation of specialized proresolving mediators contributes to the anti-inflammatory effects of aspirin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid25895638_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid25895638-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid23747022_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid23747022-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26546723_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26546723-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Additional_mechanisms">Additional mechanisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Additional mechanisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Aspirin has been shown to have at least three additional modes of action. It uncouples <a href="/wiki/Oxidative_phosphorylation" title="Oxidative phosphorylation">oxidative phosphorylation</a> in cartilaginous (and hepatic) mitochondria, by diffusing from the inner membrane space as a proton carrier back into the mitochondrial matrix, where it ionizes once again to release protons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SomasundaramS_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-SomasundaramS-137">[137]</a></sup> Aspirin buffers and transports the protons. When high doses are given, it may actually cause fever, owing to the heat released from the electron transport chain, as opposed to the antipyretic action of aspirin seen with lower doses. In addition, aspirin induces the formation of NO-radicals in the body, which have been shown in mice to have an independent mechanism of reducing inflammation. This reduced leukocyte adhesion is an important step in the immune response to infection; however, evidence is insufficient to show aspirin helps to fight infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> More recent data also suggest salicylic acid and its derivatives modulate signaling through <a href="/wiki/NF-%CE%BAB" title="NF-κB">NF-κB</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> NF-κB, a <a href="/wiki/Transcription_factor" title="Transcription factor">transcription factor</a> complex, plays a central role in many biological processes, including inflammation.</p>
<p>Aspirin is readily broken down in the body to salicylic acid, which itself has anti-inflammatory, antipyretic, and analgesic effects. In 2012, salicylic acid was found to activate <a href="/wiki/AMP-activated_protein_kinase" title="AMP-activated protein kinase">AMP-activated protein kinase</a>, which has been suggested as a possible explanation for some of the effects of both salicylic acid and aspirin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> The acetyl portion of the aspirin molecule has its own targets. Acetylation of cellular proteins is a well-established phenomenon in the regulation of protein function at the post-translational level. Aspirin is able to acetylate several other targets in addition to COX isoenzymes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> These acetylation reactions may explain many hitherto unexplained effects of aspirin.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pharmacokinetics">Pharmacokinetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Pharmacokinetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Acetylsalicylic acid is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weak_acid" title="Weak acid">weak acid</a>, and very little of it is <a href="/wiki/Acid_dissociation_constant" title="Acid dissociation constant">ionized</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Stomach" title="Stomach">stomach</a> after oral administration. Acetylsalicylic acid is quickly absorbed through the cell membrane in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acidic" title="Acidic">acidic</a> conditions of the stomach. The increased <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> and larger surface area of the <a href="/wiki/Small_intestine" title="Small intestine">small intestine</a> causes aspirin to be absorbed more slowly there, as more of it is ionised. Owing to the formation of concretions, aspirin is absorbed much more slowly during overdose, and <a href="/wiki/Blood_plasma" title="Blood plasma">plasma</a> concentrations can continue to rise for up to 24 hours after ingestion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RK_Ferguson_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-RK_Ferguson-144">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FL_Kaufman_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FL_Kaufman-145">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G_Levy_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-G_Levy-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>About 50–80% of salicylate in the blood is bound to <a href="/wiki/Albumin" title="Albumin">albumin protein</a>, while the rest remains in the active, ionized state; protein binding is concentration-dependent. Saturation of binding sites leads to more free salicylate and increased toxicity. The volume of distribution is 0.1–0.2 L/kg. Acidosis increases the volume of distribution because of enhancement of tissue penetration of salicylates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G_Levy_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-G_Levy-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>As much as 80% of therapeutic doses of salicylic acid is <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolized</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a>. <a href="/wiki/Conjugated_system" title="Conjugated system">Conjugation</a> with <a href="/wiki/Glycine" title="Glycine">glycine</a> forms <a href="/wiki/Salicyluric_acid" title="Salicyluric acid">salicyluric acid</a>, and with <a href="/wiki/Glucuronic_acid" title="Glucuronic acid">glucuronic acid</a> to form two different glucuronide esters. The conjugate with the acetyl group intact is referred to as the <i>acyl glucuronide</i>; the deacetylated conjugate is the <i>phenolic glucuronide</i>. These metabolic pathways have only a limited capacity. Small amounts of salicylic acid are also hydroxylated to <a href="/wiki/Gentisic_acid" title="Gentisic acid">gentisic acid</a>. With large salicylate doses, the kinetics switch from first-order to zero-order, as <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_pathway" title="Metabolic pathway">metabolic pathways</a> become saturated and <a href="/wiki/Kidney" title="Kidney">renal</a> excretion becomes increasingly important.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G_Levy_146-2"><a href="#cite_note-G_Levy-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>Salicylates are excreted mainly by the kidneys as salicyluric acid (75%), free salicylic acid (10%), salicylic phenol (10%), and acyl glucuronides (5%), <a href="/wiki/Gentisic_acid" title="Gentisic acid">gentisic acid</a> (&lt; 1%), and <a href="/wiki/2,3-Dihydroxybenzoic_acid" title="2,3-Dihydroxybenzoic acid">2,3-dihydroxybenzoic acid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> When small doses (less than 250 mg in an adult) are ingested, all pathways proceed by first-order kinetics, with an elimination half-life of about 2.0 h to 4.5 h.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O_Hartwig_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-O_Hartwig-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AK_Done_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-AK_Done-149">[149]</a></sup> When higher doses of salicylate are ingested (more than 4 g), the half-life becomes much longer (15 h to 30 h),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chyka2007_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chyka2007-150">[150]</a></sup> because the biotransformation pathways concerned with the formation of salicyluric acid and salicyl phenolic glucuronide become saturated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> Renal excretion of salicylic acid becomes increasingly important as the metabolic pathways become saturated, because it is extremely sensitive to changes in <a href="/wiki/Urine" title="Urine">urinary</a> pH. A 10- to 20-fold increase in renal clearance occurs when urine pH is increased from 5 to 8. The use of urinary alkalinization exploits this particular aspect of salicylate elimination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EmergMed2002-Dargan_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-EmergMed2002-Dargan-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
