</div>
</div>
<p>Medicines made from <a href="/wiki/Willow" title="Willow">willow</a> and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salicylate" title="Salicylate">salicylate</a>-rich plants appear in clay tablets from ancient <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumer</a> as well as the <a href="/wiki/Ebers_Papyrus" title="Ebers Papyrus">Ebers Papyrus</a> from ancient Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeffreys2008_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffreys2008-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:8–13</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACS_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-ACS-8">[8]</a></sup> Hippocrates referred to their use of salicylic tea to reduce fevers around 400 BC, and were part of the pharmacopoeia of Western medicine in <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical antiquity</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACS_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-ACS-8">[8]</a></sup> Willow bark extract became recognized for its specific effects on fever, pain and inflammation in the mid-eighteenth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChemHer_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChemHer-153">[153]</a></sup> By the nineteenth century pharmacists were experimenting with and prescribing a variety of chemicals related to <a href="/wiki/Salicylic_acid" title="Salicylic acid">salicylic acid</a>, the active component of willow extract.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeffreys2008_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffreys2008-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:46–55</sup></p>
<p>In 1853, chemist <a href="/wiki/Charles_Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Gerhardt" title="Charles Frédéric Gerhardt">Charles Frédéric Gerhardt</a> treated <a href="/wiki/Acetyl_chloride" title="Acetyl chloride">acetyl chloride</a> with <a href="/wiki/Sodium_salicylate" title="Sodium salicylate">sodium salicylate</a> to produce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acetylsalicylic_acid" title="Acetylsalicylic acid">acetylsalicylic acid</a> for the first time;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeffreys2008_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffreys2008-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:46–48</sup> in the second half of the nineteenth century, other academic chemists established the compound's chemical structure and devised more efficient methods of synthesis. In 1897, scientists at the drug and dye firm <a href="/wiki/Bayer" title="Bayer">Bayer</a> began investigating acetylsalicylic acid as a less-irritating replacement for standard common salicylate medicines, and identified a new way to synthesize it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeffreys2008_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffreys2008-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:69–75</sup> By 1899, Bayer had dubbed this drug Aspirin and was selling it around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MannPlummer1991_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-MannPlummer1991-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:27</sup> The word <i>Aspirin</i> was Bayer's brand name, rather than the generic name of the drug; however, Bayer's rights to the trademark were lost or sold in many countries. Aspirin's popularity grew over the first half of the twentieth century leading to fierce competition with the proliferation of aspirin brands and products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACS_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-ACS-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Aspirin's popularity declined after the development of <a href="/wiki/Paracetamol" title="Paracetamol">acetaminophen/paracetamol</a> in 1956 and <a href="/wiki/Ibuprofen" title="Ibuprofen">ibuprofen</a> in 1962. In the 1960s and 1970s, <a href="/wiki/John_Vane" title="John Vane">John Vane</a> and others discovered the basic mechanism of aspirin's effects,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeffreys2008_6-6"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffreys2008-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:226–231</sup> while clinical trials and other studies from the 1960s to the 1980s established aspirin's efficacy as an anti-clotting agent that reduces the risk of clotting diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeffreys2008_6-7"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffreys2008-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:247–257</sup> Aspirin sales revived considerably in the last decades of the twentieth century, and remain strong in the twenty-first with widespread use as a preventive treatment for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_attack" title="Heart attack">heart attacks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">strokes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeffreys2008_6-8"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffreys2008-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:267–269</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trademark">Trademark</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Trademark">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Due to allowing the use of "Aspirin" for years by other manufacturing chemists, despite the trademark-infringing nature of the use, and its own failure to use the name for its own product when it began selling direct, Bayer lost its trademark in the United States in 1918, affirmed by court appeal in 1921.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> Today, aspirin is a <a href="/wiki/Generic_trademark" title="Generic trademark">generic word</a> in Australia, France, India, Ireland, New Zealand, Pakistan, Jamaica, Colombia, the Philippines, South Africa, Ghana, the United Kingdom and the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> Aspirin, with a capital "A", remains a registered trademark of Bayer in Germany, Canada, Mexico, and in over 80 other countries, where the trademark is owned by Bayer, using acetylsalicylic acid in all markets, but using different packaging and physical aspects for each.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>Aspirin is the <a href="/wiki/British_Approved_Name" title="British Approved Name">British Approved Name</a> (BAN) and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Adopted_Name" title="United States Adopted Name">United States Adopted Name</a> (USAN).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Compendial_status">Compendial status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Compendial status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/United_States_Pharmacopeia" title="United States Pharmacopeia">United States Pharmacopeia</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asa_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-asa-158">[158]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/British_Pharmacopoeia" title="British Pharmacopoeia">British Pharmacopoeia</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ibp_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-ibp-159">[159]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Veterinary_medicine">Veterinary medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aspirin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Veterinary medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Aspirin is sometimes used for pain relief or as an anticoagulant in veterinary medicine, primarily in dogs and sometimes horses, although newer medications with fewer side effects are generally used instead.</p>
<p>Both dogs and horses are susceptible to the gastrointestinal side effects associated with salicylates, but it is a convenient treatment for arthritis in older dogs, and has shown some promise in cases of <a href="/wiki/Laminitis" title="Laminitis">laminitis</a> in horses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources_(medicine)" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources (medicine)"><span title="Material near this tag may rely on an unreliable or less reliable medical source. (August 2016)">unreliable medical source?</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CambridgeH_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-CambridgeH-161">[161]</a></sup> It is no longer commonly used for cases of laminitis, as it could be counterproductive for treatment. Aspirin should be used in animals only under the direct supervision of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Veterinarian" title="Veterinarian">veterinarian</a>; in particular, cats lack the <a href="/wiki/Glucuronide" title="Glucuronide">glucuronide</a> conjugates that aid in the excretion of aspirin, making it potentially toxic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> No clinical signs of toxicosis occurred when cats were given 25 mg/kg of aspirin every 48 hours for 4 weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> The dose recommended in cats for relief of pain and fever is 10 mg/kg every 48 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup></p>

<li id="cite_note-154"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-154">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite><i>Bayer Co. v. United Drug Co.</i></cite>, <a class="external text" href="https://cyber.law.harvard.edu/metaschool/fisher/domain/tmcases/bayer.htm" rel="nofollow">272 F. 505</a>, p.512 (S.D.N.Y 1921) (“Disregarding this, however, it was too late in the autumn of 1915 to reclaim the word which had already passed into the public domain. If the consuming public had once learned to know 'Aspirin' as the accepted name for the drug, perhaps it is true that an extended course of education might have added to it some proprietary meaning, but it would be very difficult to prove that it had been done in 17 months, and in any case the plaintiff does not try to prove it. [...] Yet, had it not been indifferent to the results of selling to the consumer, it could have protected itself just as well at the time when consumers began to buy directly as in 1915. Nothing would have been easier than to insist that the tablet makers should market the drug in small tin boxes bearing the plaintiff's name, or to take over the sale just as it did later. Instead of this, they allowed the manufacturing chemists to build up this part of the demand without regard to the trademark. Having made that bed, they must be content to lie in it. Hence it appears to me that nothing happening between October 1915, and March 1917, will serve to turn the word into a trademark.”).</span></li>
